href="https://pt-corp.storage.yandexcloud.net/upload/corporate/ww-en/images/esc/article_316844/image61.png"/><meta name="next-size-adjust" content=""/><title>Higaisa or Winnti? APT41 backdoors, old and new</title><meta name="description" content="
	The PT Expert Security Center regularly spots emerging threats to information security, including both previously known and newly discovered malware. During such monitoring in May 2020, we detected several samples of new malware that at first glance would seem to belong to the Higaisa group. But detailed analysis pointed to the Winnti group (also known as APT41, per FireEye) of Chinese origin. Subsequent monitoring led us to discover a number of new malware samples used by the group in recent attacks. These include various droppers, loaders, and injectors; Crosswalk, ShadowPad, and PlugX backdoors; and samples of a previously undescribed backdoor that we have dubbed FunnySwitch. We can confidently state that some of these attacks were directed at a number of organizations in Russia and Hong Kong.


	In this article, we will share the results of our investigation of these samples and related network infrastructure, as well as overlaps with previously described attacks.

APT41 backdoors, old and new</h4></div><div class="components_actions___wz9f"><button class="Button_button__W_E0l Button_plain__oXQ1P Button_plain__oXQ1P" type="button"><div class="InnerBox_button-box__dG_t7 InnerBox_button-box_small__u3OgN"><span class="truncate weight_regular__5MYqU size_xs__7K9zV margin_none__1sjaB color_inherit__bnxZL uppercase whitespace-nowrap">Share</span><div class="Icon_icon__IyoOP Icon_xs__BcEb6"><svg role="img" focusable="false" aria-hidden="true" viewBox="0 0 16 16" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" fill="none"><path fill="none" stroke="currentColor" d="M2.974 11.156c.204-1.738.804-2.911 1.508-3.71.879-.996 1.964-1.458 2.788-1.672a5.25 5.25 0 0 1 1.183-.179H9.58v-.499l.002-1.31 3.39 3.24.08.078-3.47 3.32V8.611H8.277c-2.9 0-4.502 1.441-5.302 2.544Zm6.61-7.544Z"></path></svg></div></div></button></div></div><div class="components_progress__cV20H"><span class="components_line__1Acej"></span></div></div><div class="tablet:gap-5 fixed bottom-0 right-0 z-40 flex flex-col gap-3 p-3 opacity-0" data-theme="dark"><button type="button" class="IconButton_icon__uMEG7 IconButton_lg__PUqDy IconButton_outlined__kMeaW IconButton_glassy__RII_U shadow-icon-button-glassy pointer-events-auto"><div style="color:rgb(151, 161, 176)" class="Icon_icon__IyoOP Icon_md___TsqE"><svg role="img" focusable="false" aria-hidden="true" viewBox="0 0 20 20" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" fill="none"><path stroke="currentColor" stroke-linecap="round" d="m16 13-5.646-5.646a.5.5 0 0 0-.708 0L4 13"></path></svg></div></button></div><article><div class="flex flex-col gap-10 laptop:gap-15"><div class="GridLayout_grid-wrap__UGXQb GridLayout_grid-gap-medium__a2T51 ArticleContentContainer_container__0vDuu content-item"><div class="col-span-4 mobile:col-span-4 tablet:col-span-7 tablet-landscape:col-span-7 laptop:col-span-6 flex flex-col"><div class="HtmlBlock_article-content__4o6_U break-words HtmlBlock_deprecated__3I2_a"><article>
<p class="weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_secondary-plus__47VrA">The <a target="_blank" rel="nofollow noreferrer" href="https://www.ptsecurity.com/ww-en/analytics/pt-esc-threat-intelligence/" class="linkStyles_links-gray__auxKT weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_links-gray__IEMoh linkStyles_underlined__4E3jf" title="https://www.ptsecurity.com/ww-en/analytics/pt-esc-threat-intelligence/">PT Expert Security Center</a> regularly spots emerging threats to information security, including both previously known and newly discovered malware. During such monitoring in May 2020, we detected several samples of new malware that at first glance would seem to belong to the Higaisa group. But detailed analysis pointed to the <a target="_blank" rel="nofollow noreferrer" href="https://www.ptsecurity.com/ww-en/analytics/pt-esc-threat-intelligence/shadowpad-new-activity-from-the-winnti-group/" class="linkStyles_links-gray__auxKT weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_links-gray__IEMoh linkStyles_underlined__4E3jf" title="https://www.ptsecurity.com/ww-en/analytics/pt-esc-threat-intelligence/shadowpad-new-activity-from-the-winnti-group/">Winnti group (also known as APT41, per FireEye)</a> of Chinese origin. Subsequent monitoring led us to discover a number of new malware samples used by the group in recent attacks. These include various droppers, loaders, and injectors; Crosswalk, ShadowPad, and PlugX backdoors; and samples of a previously undescribed backdoor that we have dubbed FunnySwitch. We can confidently state that some of these attacks were directed at a number of organizations in Russia and Hong Kong.</p>
<p class="weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_secondary-plus__47VrA">In this article, we will share the results of our investigation of these samples and related network infrastructure, as well as overlaps with previously described attacks.</p>

<h2 id="id1" class="break-words size_m__P_9U_ color_primary__z_39p margin_none__1sjaB">1. Higaisa shortcuts</h2>
<p class="weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_secondary-plus__47VrA">The first attack dates to May 12, 2020. At the core of the attack is an archive named <em>Project link and New copyright policy.rar</em> (75cd8d24030a3160b1f49f1b46257f9d6639433214a10564d432b74cc8c4d020). The archive contains a bait PDF document (Zeplin Copyright Policy.pdf) plus the folder <b>All tort&#x27;s projects - Web lnks</b> with two shortcuts:</p>
<ul class="listStyles_list__w6p69 listStyles_ul__CFFG0 weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_secondary-plus__47VrA margin_xs__lb5TS">
<li>Conversations - iOS - Swipe Icons - Zeplin.lnk</li>
<li>Tokbox icon - Odds and Ends - iOS - Zeplin.lnk</li>
</ul>
<p class="weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_secondary-plus__47VrA">The structure of malicious shortcuts resembles the sample 20200308-sitrep-48-covid-19.pdf.lnk <a target="_blank" rel="nofollow noreferrer" href="https://www.ptsecurity.com/ww-en/analytics/pt-esc-threat-intelligence/covid-19-and-new-year-greetings-the-higaisa-group/" class="linkStyles_links-gray__auxKT weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_links-gray__IEMoh linkStyles_underlined__4E3jf" title="https://www.ptsecurity.com/ww-en/analytics/pt-esc-threat-intelligence/covid-19-and-new-year-greetings-the-higaisa-group/">spread by the Higaisa group</a> in March 2020.</p>
<figure>
    <img src="https://pt-corp.storage.yandexcloud.net/upload/corporate/ww-en/images/esc/article_316844/image1.png" alt="Comparing command lines in the covid-19 and Zeplin shortcuts"/>
    <figcaption>Figure 1. Comparing command lines in the covid-19 and Zeplin shortcuts</figcaption>
</figure>
<p class="weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_secondary-plus__47VrA">The mechanism for initial infection is fundamentally the same: trying to open either of the shortcuts leads to running a command that extracts a Base64-encoded CAB archive from the body of the LNK file, after which the archive is unpacked to a temporary folder. Further actions are performed with the help of an extracted JS script.</p>
<figure>
    <img src="https://pt-corp.storage.yandexcloud.net/upload/corporate/ww-en/images/esc/article_316844/image2.png" alt="Contents of script 34fDFkfSD32.js"/>
    <figcaption>Figure 2. Contents of script 34fDFkfSD32.js</figcaption>
</figure>
<p class="weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_secondary-plus__47VrA">But here is where the similarity with the sample described in our Higaisa report ends: instead, this script copies the payload to the folder C:\Users\Public\Downloads, achieves persistence by adding itself to the startup folder and adding a scheduler task, and runs the payload. The script also sends the output of ipconfig in a POST request to http://zeplin.atwebpages[.]com/inter.php.</p>
<p class="weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_secondary-plus__47VrA">The command run by the shortcut also contains the opening of a URL file extracted from the archive. The name of the URL file and the target address depend on which shortcut is opened:</p>
<ul class="listStyles_list__w6p69 listStyles_ul__CFFG0 weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_secondary-plus__47VrA margin_xs__lb5TS">
<li><p class="weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_secondary-plus__47VrA">Conversations - iOS - Swipe Icons - Zeplin.url goes to:</p>
	<p class="weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_secondary-plus__47VrA">https://app.zeplin.io/project/5b5741802f3131c3a63057a4/screen/5b589f697e44cee37e0e61df</p>
</li>
<li><p class="weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_secondary-plus__47VrA">Tokbox icon - Odds and Ends - iOS - Zeplin.url goes to:</p>
	<p class="weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_secondary-plus__47VrA">https://app.zeplin.io/project/5c161c03fde4d550a251e20a/screen/5cef98986801a41be35122bb.</p>
</li>
</ul>
<p class="weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_secondary-plus__47VrA">This is the only difference between the two LNK files. In both cases, the target page is hosted on Zeplin, a legitimate service for collaboration between designers and developers, and requires logging in to view.</p>
<p class="weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_secondary-plus__47VrA">The payload consists of two files:</p>
<ul class="listStyles_list__w6p69 listStyles_ul__CFFG0 weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_secondary-plus__47VrA margin_xs__lb5TS">
<li><p class="weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_secondary-plus__47VrA">svchast.exe</p>
	<p class="weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_secondary-plus__47VrA">It functions as a simple local shellcode loader. The shellcode read from a fixed path. Before starting, the loader checks the current year: 2018, 2019, 2020, or 2021.</p>
<figure>
    <img src="https://pt-corp.storage.yandexcloud.net/upload/corporate/ww-en/images/esc/article_316844/image3.png" alt="Main function in svchast.exe"/>
    <figcaption>Figure 3. Main function in svchast.exe</figcaption>
</figure>
</li><li>
	<p class="weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_secondary-plus__47VrA">3t54dE3r.tmp</p>
	<p class="weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_secondary-plus__47VrA">The shellcode containing the main payload is the Crosswalk backdoor.</p>
</li>
</ul>
<p class="weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_secondary-plus__47VrA">On May 30, 2020, a new malicious archive, CV_Colliers.rar (df999d24bde96decdbb65287ca0986db98f73b4ed477e18c3ef100064bceba6d), was detected. It had two shortcuts:</p>
<ul class="listStyles_list__w6p69 listStyles_ul__CFFG0 weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_secondary-plus__47VrA margin_xs__lb5TS">
<li>Curriculum Vitae_WANG LEI_Hong Kong Polytechnic University.pdf.lnk</li>
<li>International English Language Testing System certificate.pdf.lnk</li>
</ul>
<p class="weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_secondary-plus__47VrA">Their structure fully matched that of the samples from May 12. In this case, the bait consisted of PDF documents with a CV and IELTS certificate. Depending on which shortcut was opened, the output of ipconfig was sent to one of two addresses: http://goodhk.azurewebsites[.]net/inter.php or http://sixindent.epizy[.]com/inter.php.</p>
<p class="weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_secondary-plus__47VrA">Note that all three intermediate C2 servers are on third-level domains on a free hosting service. When accessed in a browser, each displays a different decoy page:</p>
<figure>
    <img src="https://pt-corp.storage.yandexcloud.net/upload/corporate/ww-en/images/esc/article_316844/image4.png" alt="Page at zeplin.atwebpages_com"/>
    <figcaption>Figure 4. Page at zeplin.atwebpages_com</figcaption>
</figure>
<figure>
    <img src="https://pt-corp.storage.yandexcloud.net/upload/corporate/ww-en/images/esc/article_316844/image5.png" alt="Page at goodhk.azurewebsites_net"/>
    <figcaption>Figure 5. Page at goodhk.azurewebsites_net</figcaption>
</figure>
<figure>
    <img src="https://pt-corp.storage.yandexcloud.net/upload/corporate/ww-en/images/esc/article_316844/image6.png" alt="Page at sixindent.epizy_com"/>
    <figcaption>Figure 6. Page at sixindent.epizy_com</figcaption>
</figure>
<p class="weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_secondary-plus__47VrA">These servers do not play a major role in the functioning of the malware; their precise purpose remains unknown. It may be that the malware authors used this to monitor the success of the initial stages of infection, or else tried to lead security teams &quot;off the scent&quot; by masking the malware as a more minor threat.</p>
<h3 id="id1-1" class="break-words size_s__3_Wdt color_primary__z_39p margin_none__1sjaB">1.1 Attribution</h3>
<p class="weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_secondary-plus__47VrA">These attacks have been studied in detail by <a target="_blank" rel="nofollow noreferrer" href="https://blog.malwarebytes.com/threat-analysis/2020/06/higaisa/" class="linkStyles_links-gray__auxKT weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_links-gray__IEMoh linkStyles_underlined__4E3jf" title="https://blog.malwarebytes.com/threat-analysis/2020/06/higaisa/">Malwarebytes</a> and <a target="_blank" rel="nofollow noreferrer" href="https://www.zscaler.com/blogs/research/return-higaisa-apt" class="linkStyles_links-gray__auxKT weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_links-gray__IEMoh linkStyles_underlined__4E3jf" title="https://www.zscaler.com/blogs/research/return-higaisa-apt">Zscaler</a>. Based on the similarity of the infection chains, researchers classify them as belonging to the Higaisa group.</p>
<p class="weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_secondary-plus__47VrA">However, detailed analysis of the shellcode demonstrates that the samples actually belong to the Crosswalk malware family. Crosswalk appeared no later than 2017 and was mentioned for the first time in <a target="_blank" rel="nofollow noreferrer" href="https://content.fireeye.com/apt-41/rpt-apt41/" class="linkStyles_links-gray__auxKT weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_links-gray__IEMoh linkStyles_underlined__4E3jf" title="https://content.fireeye.com/apt-41/rpt-apt41/">a FireEye report</a> on the activities of the APT41 (Winnti) group.</p>
<figure>
    <img src="https://pt-corp.storage.yandexcloud.net/upload/corporate/ww-en/images/esc/article_316844/image7.png" alt="From the FireEye report"/>
    <figcaption>Figure 7. From the FireEye report</figcaption>
</figure>
<figure>
    <img src="https://pt-corp.storage.yandexcloud.net/upload/corporate/ww-en/images/esc/article_316844/image8.png" alt="Fragment of shellcode from 3t54dE3r.tmp"/>
    <figcaption>Figure 8. Fragment of shellcode from 3t54dE3r.tmp</figcaption>
</figure>
<p class="weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_secondary-plus__47VrA">The network infrastructure of the samples overlaps with previously known APT41 infrastructure: at the IP address of one of the C2 servers, we find an SSL certificate with SHA-1 value of b8cff709950cfa86665363d9553532db9922265c, which is also found at IP address 67.229.97[.]229, referenced in <a target="_blank" rel="nofollow noreferrer" href="https://go.crowdstrike.com/rs/281-OBQ-266/images/Report2019GlobalThreatReport.pdf" class="linkStyles_links-gray__auxKT weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_links-gray__IEMoh linkStyles_underlined__4E3jf" title="https://go.crowdstrike.com/rs/281-OBQ-266/images/Report2019GlobalThreatReport.pdf">a 2018 CrowdStrike report</a>. Going further, we can find domains from <a target="_blank" rel="nofollow noreferrer" href="https://media.kasperskycontenthub.com/wp-content/uploads/sites/43/2018/03/20134508/winnti-more-than-just-a-game-130410.pdf" class="linkStyles_links-gray__auxKT weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_links-gray__IEMoh linkStyles_underlined__4E3jf" title="https://media.kasperskycontenthub.com/wp-content/uploads/sites/43/2018/03/20134508/winnti-more-than-just-a-game-130410.pdf">a Kaspersky report</a> written in 2013.</p>
<figure>
    <img src="https://pt-corp.storage.yandexcloud.net/upload/corporate/ww-en/images/esc/article_316844/image9.png" alt="Fragment of network infrastructure"/>
    <figcaption>Figure 9. Fragment of network infrastructure</figcaption>
</figure>
<p class="weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_secondary-plus__47VrA">All this leads us to conclude that these LNK file attacks were performed by Winnti (APT41), which &quot;borrowed&quot; this shortcut technique from Higaisa.</p>
<h3 id="id1-2" class="break-words size_s__3_Wdt color_primary__z_39p margin_none__1sjaB">1.2 Crosswalk</h3>
<p class="weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_secondary-plus__47VrA">Crosswalk is a modular backdoor implemented in shellcode. The main component connects to a C2 server, collects and sends system information, and contains functionality for installing and running up to 20 additional modules received from the server as shellcode.</p>
<p class="weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_secondary-plus__47VrA">The information collected by the module includes:</p>
<ul class="listStyles_list__w6p69 listStyles_ul__CFFG0 weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_secondary-plus__47VrA margin_xs__lb5TS">
<li>OS uptime</li>
<li>Network adapter IP addresses</li>
<li>MAC address of one of the adapters</li>
<li>Operating system version and whether it is 32-bit or 64-bit</li>
<li>Username</li>
<li>Computer name</li>
<li>Name of running module</li>
<li>PID</li>
<li>Shellcode version and whether it is 32-bit or 64-bit</li>
</ul>
<p class="weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_secondary-plus__47VrA">(The shellcode supports both 32 and 64 bits.) It has two-part version numbers; we found ones including 1.0, 1.10, 1.21, 1.22, 1.25, and 2.0.</p>
<p class="weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_secondary-plus__47VrA">For more detailed analysis of one version of Crosswalk, see the <a target="_blank" rel="nofollow noreferrer" href="https://www.carbonblack.com/blog/cb-threat-analysis-unit-technical-analysis-of-crosswalk/" class="linkStyles_links-gray__auxKT weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_links-gray__IEMoh linkStyles_underlined__4E3jf" title="https://www.carbonblack.com/blog/cb-threat-analysis-unit-technical-analysis-of-crosswalk/">VMware CarbonBlack investigation</a>. Based on version 1.25 (8e6945ae06dd849b9db0c2983bca82de1dddbf79afb371aa88da71c19c44c996), which was used in the attacks with LNK files, here we will describe the networking aspects of the malware in more detail.</p>
<p class="weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_secondary-plus__47VrA">Crosswalk has broad capabilities for connecting to C2 servers. The network configuration for this particular sample is at the end of the shellcode and is XOR encrypted with a 16-byte key. The data structure is as follows:</p>
<ul class="listStyles_list__w6p69 listStyles_ul__CFFG0 weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_secondary-plus__47VrA margin_xs__lb5TS">
<li>Configuration size (4 bytes)</li>
<li>Key (16 bytes)</li>
<li>Encrypted configuration</li>
</ul>
<p class="weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_secondary-plus__47VrA">The configuration, in turn, contains the following fields:</p>
<ul class="listStyles_list__w6p69 listStyles_ul__CFFG0 weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_secondary-plus__47VrA margin_xs__lb5TS">
<li>0x0 heartbeat interval (in seconds)</li>
<li>0x4 reconnect interval (in seconds)</li>
<li>0x8 bitmask for days of the week when connections may be made</li>
<li>0xC (inclusive) lower bound for time of day when connections may be made</li>
<li>0x10 (non-inclusive) upper bound for time of day when connections may be made</li>
<li>0x14 proxy port</li>
<li>0x18 proxy type</li>
<li>0x1C proxy host</li>
<li>0x9C proxy username</li>
<li>0x11C proxy password</li>
<li>0x19C number of C2 servers</li>
<li>0x1A0 array of structures of C2 servers</li>
</ul>
<p class="weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_secondary-plus__47VrA">A C2 server structure consists of the following fields:</p>
<ul class="listStyles_list__w6p69 listStyles_ul__CFFG0 weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_secondary-plus__47VrA margin_xs__lb5TS">
<li>0x0 connection type</li>
<li>0x4 port</li>
<li>0x8 whether DNS name resolution is necessary (yes/no)</li>
<li>0xC length of hostname</li>
<li>0x10 hostname</li>
</ul>
<p class="weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_secondary-plus__47VrA">Before attempting to connect, the backdoor checks whether the current day of the week and time match those allowed in the configuration. Then, one after the other, it tries combinations of possible proxy servers (any indicated in the configuration plus system proxies) and C2 servers until it connects successfully.</p>
<p class="weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_secondary-plus__47VrA">The communication protocol used between the backdoor and C2 server can be separated logically into two levels:</p>
<ol class="listStyles_list__w6p69 listStyles_ol__ZnTfe weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_secondary-plus__47VrA margin_xs__lb5TS">
<li>Application-level protocol</li>
<li>Transport-level protocol</li>
</ol>
<p class="weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_secondary-plus__47VrA">On the application level, messages consist of the following fields:</p>
<ul class="listStyles_list__w6p69 listStyles_ul__CFFG0 weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_secondary-plus__47VrA margin_xs__lb5TS">
<li>FakeTLS header consisting of 5 bytes:
<ul class="listStyles_list__w6p69 listStyles_ul__CFFG0 weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_secondary-plus__47VrA margin_xs__lb5TS">
<li>Entry type and protocol version (3 bytes). For the client these always equal 17 03 01; for the server, they have random values.</li>
<li>Data length, not including header (2 bytes)</li>
</ul></li>
<li>Message contents:
<ul class="listStyles_list__w6p69 listStyles_ul__CFFG0 weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_secondary-plus__47VrA margin_xs__lb5TS">
<li>Command ID (4 bytes, little-endian)</li>
<li>Command data size (4 bytes, little-endian)</li>
<li>Client ID (36 bytes), generated based on the UUID when the backdoor starts operation</li>
<li>Command data</li>
</ul></li>
</ul>
<p class="weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_secondary-plus__47VrA">The first two client–server and server–client messages have command IDs 0x65 and 0x64, respectively. They contain the data that will then be used to generate the client and server session keys. The key generation algorithm is detailed in a <a target="_blank" rel="nofollow noreferrer" href="https://www.zscaler.com/blogs/research/return-higaisa-apt" class="linkStyles_links-gray__auxKT weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_links-gray__IEMoh linkStyles_underlined__4E3jf" title="https://www.zscaler.com/blogs/research/return-higaisa-apt">Zscaler report</a>. For all subsequent messages, the content (not including the FakeTLS header) is transferred in the corresponding encrypted session key. AES-128 is the encryption algorithm used.</p>
<p class="weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_secondary-plus__47VrA">The transport-level protocol depends on the connection type indicated in the configuration. Four protocols are supported:</p>
<ol class="listStyles_list__w6p69 listStyles_ol__ZnTfe weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_secondary-plus__47VrA margin_xs__lb5TS">
<li>Standard TCP connection
	<p class="weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_secondary-plus__47VrA">Application-level messages are sent unchanged as TCP segments.</p>
</li>
<li>Equivalent to HTTP Long Polling
	<p class="weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_secondary-plus__47VrA">The client creates two TCP connections. The first will be used to get packets from the server, and the second to send them.</p>
	<p class="weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_secondary-plus__47VrA">During the first connection, a GET request is sent to the C2 server. The server replies with headers with code 200 and Content-Length: 524288000. The subsequent stream of application-level messages from the server to the client is sent as the body of an HTTP response.</p>
<figure>
    <img src="https://pt-corp.storage.yandexcloud.net/upload/corporate/ww-en/images/esc/article_316844/image10.png" alt="First HTTP connection with C2"/>
    <figcaption>Figure 10. First HTTP connection with C2</figcaption>
</figure>
<p class="weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_secondary-plus__47VrA">After the correct response headers are received, the malware establishes a second connection to the same port, where a POST request is made. The header <em>dCy</em> is generated by the client based on the UUID and, it would seem, serves as the session ID that links the two connections. After receipt of a response with code 200, subsequent messages from the client to the server are sent using separate POST requests.</p>
<figure>
    <img src="https://pt-corp.storage.yandexcloud.net/upload/corporate/ww-en/images/esc/article_316844/image11.png" alt="Second HTTP connection with C2"/>
    <figcaption>Figure 11. Second HTTP connection with C2</figcaption>
</figure></li>
<li>Duplication of socket with TLS connection
	<p class="weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_secondary-plus__47VrA">The client establishes a TCP connection and sends an HTTPS request like the following one:</p>
<div class="bg-skin-content-general-reversed laptop:rounded-xl rounded-lg py-8 pl-5 pr-8 text-[#ABB2BF] HtmlBlock_code__Ng4fC"><div class="pr-15 relative flex gap-5"><pre class="w-full whitespace-pre-wrap break-words"><div class="code">GET /msdn.cpp HTTP/1.1
Connection: Keep-Alive
User-Agent: WinHTTP/1.1
Content-Length: 4294967295
Host: 149.28.152[.]196</div></pre><div class="absolute bottom-0 right-0" data-theme-direction="reversed"><button type="button" class="IconButton_icon__uMEG7 IconButton_sm__bK6zi IconButton_outlined__kMeaW"><div class="Icon_icon__IyoOP Icon_sm__5FkXp"><svg role="img" focusable="false" aria-hidden="true" viewBox="0 0 20 20" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" fill="none"><path stroke="currentColor" d="M6 3a2 2 0 0 1 2-2h8a2 2 0 0 1 2 2v10a2 2 0 0 1-2 2H8a2 2 0 0 1-2-2V3Z"></path><path stroke="currentColor" d="M14 17a2 2 0 0 1-2 2H4a2 2 0 0 1-2-2V7a2 2 0 0 1 2-2"></path></svg></div></button></div></div></div>
<p class="weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_secondary-plus__47VrA">The HTTPS connection is not used again. Subsequent messages are exchanged in the <b>original TCP connection (without TLS encryption)</b>. Subsequent communication between the client and server occurs via protocol 1, except for when, at the beginning of the session, the client sends two packets with the FakeTLS header, which starts with the sequence 17 03 01. The first packet always has length 0. The second has length 0x3A, 0x3C, 0x3E, or 0x40 and contains random bytes. We were unable to determine the purpose of these packets.</p>
<figure>
    <img src="https://pt-corp.storage.yandexcloud.net/upload/corporate/ww-en/images/esc/article_316844/image12.png" alt="Additional packets with FakeTLS header"/>
    <figcaption>Figure 12. Additional packets with FakeTLS header</figcaption>
</figure>
</li>
<li><a target="_blank" rel="nofollow noreferrer" href="https://github.com/skywind3000/kcp/blob/master/README.en.md" class="linkStyles_links-gray__auxKT weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_links-gray__IEMoh linkStyles_underlined__4E3jf" title="https://github.com/skywind3000/kcp/blob/master/README.en.md">KCP protocol</a>
	<p class="weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_secondary-plus__47VrA">This protocol can be implemented on top of any other protocol (including UDP) to ensure quick and reliable data transfer. The Crosswalk client uses KCP on top of a TCP connection: KCP protocol data is added to application-level messages that are then sent as TCP segments.</p>
<figure>
    <img src="https://pt-corp.storage.yandexcloud.net/upload/corporate/ww-en/images/esc/article_316844/image13.png" alt="Crosswalk message with KCP headers (highlighted in yellow)"/>
    <figcaption>Figure 13. Crosswalk message with KCP headers (highlighted in yellow)</figcaption>
</figure>
</li>
</ol>
<p class="weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_secondary-plus__47VrA">Note that in the Crosswalk samples we detected, none of the samples used the KCP protocol in practice. But the code contains a full-fledged implementation of this protocol, which could be used in other attacks: the developers would simply need to set this connection type in the configuration.</p>
<p class="weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_secondary-plus__47VrA">The diversity of protocols and techniques would seem to protect the backdoor from network traffic inspection.</p>
<h2 id="id2" class="break-words size_m__P_9U_ color_primary__z_39p margin_none__1sjaB">2. Loaders and injectors</h2>
<p class="weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_secondary-plus__47VrA">Investigation of network infrastructure and monitoring of new Crosswalk samples put us onto the scent of other malicious objects containing Crosswalk shellcode as their payload. We can categorize these objects into two groups: local shellcode loaders and injectors. Some of the samples in both groups are also obfuscated with VMProtect.</p>
<h3 id="id2-1" class="break-words size_s__3_Wdt color_primary__z_39p margin_none__1sjaB">2.1 Injectors</h3>
<figure>
    <img src="https://pt-corp.storage.yandexcloud.net/upload/corporate/ww-en/images/esc/article_316844/image14.png" alt="Code for injecting shellcode into a running process"/>
    <figcaption>Figure 14. Code for injecting shellcode into a running process</figcaption>
</figure>
<p class="weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_secondary-plus__47VrA">The injectors contain typical code that obtains SeDebugPrivilege, finds the PID of the target process, and injects shellcode into it. Depending on the sample, explorer.exe and winlogon.exe are the target processes.</p>
<p class="weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_secondary-plus__47VrA">The samples we found contain one of three payloads:</p>
<ul class="listStyles_list__w6p69 listStyles_ul__CFFG0 weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_secondary-plus__47VrA margin_xs__lb5TS">
<li>Crosswalk</li>
<li>Metasploit stager</li>
<li>FunnySwitch (discussed later in this report)</li>
</ul>
<p class="weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_secondary-plus__47VrA">Crosswalk and FunnySwitch shellcode is located in the data sections &quot;as-is,&quot; while the samples with Metasploit show additional XOR encryption with the key &quot;jj1&quot;.</p>
<h3 id="id2-2" class="break-words size_s__3_Wdt color_primary__z_39p margin_none__1sjaB">2.2 Local shellcode loaders</h3>
<p class="weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_secondary-plus__47VrA">The main function of the malware is to extract shellcode and run it in an active process. The malware samples belong to one of two categories, based on the source of shellcode that they use: in the original executable or in an external file in the same directory.</p>
<p class="weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_secondary-plus__47VrA">Most of the loaders start by checking the current year, much like the samples from the LNK file attacks.</p>
<figure>
    <img src="https://pt-corp.storage.yandexcloud.net/upload/corporate/ww-en/images/esc/article_316844/image15.png" alt="Code of the loader&#x27;s main function"/>
    <figcaption>Figure 15. Code of the loader&#x27;s main function</figcaption>
</figure>
<p class="weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_secondary-plus__47VrA">After the malware finds the API functions it needs, it decrypts the string Global\0EluZTRM3Kye4Hv65IGfoaX9sSP7VA with the <a target="_blank" rel="nofollow noreferrer" href="https://tools.ietf.org/html/rfc7539" class="linkStyles_links-gray__auxKT weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_links-gray__IEMoh linkStyles_underlined__4E3jf" title="https://tools.ietf.org/html/rfc7539">ChaCha20</a> algorithm. In one older version, to prevent being run twice the loader creates a mutex with the name Global\5hJ4YfUoyHlwVMnS1qZkd2tEmz7GPbB. But in recent samples, the decrypted string is not used in any way. Perhaps part of the code was accidentally deleted during the development process.</p>
<p class="weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_secondary-plus__47VrA">Another artifact found in some samples is the unused string <em>CSPELOADKISSYOU</em>. Its purpose remains unclear.</p>
<figure>
    <img src="https://pt-corp.storage.yandexcloud.net/upload/corporate/ww-en/images/esc/article_316844/image16.png" alt="String "/>
    <figcaption>Figure 16. String &quot;CSPELOADKISSYOU&quot; in data section</figcaption>
</figure>
<p class="weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_secondary-plus__47VrA">In the self-contained loaders, the shellcode is located in a PE file overlay. The shellcode is stored in a curious way: data starts from 0x60 bytes of the header, followed by the (encrypted) shellcode. The data length is stored at offset –0x24 from the end of the executable. The header always starts with the PL signature. The other header data is used for decryption: a 32-byte key is located at offset 0x28 and a 12-byte nonce for the ChaCha20 algorithm is at offset 0x50.</p>
<figure>
    <img src="https://pt-corp.storage.yandexcloud.net/upload/corporate/ww-en/images/esc/article_316844/image17.png" alt="Handling of PL shellcode in the loader body (ChaCha20)"/>
    <figcaption>Figure 17. Handling of PL shellcode in the loader body (ChaCha20)</figcaption>
</figure>
<p class="weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_secondary-plus__47VrA">The ChaCha20 implementation is not always present: some of the samples use Microsoft CryptoAPI with AES-128-CBC for encryption. We can also find key information here in the structure of the PL shellcode: at offset 0x28, there are 32 bytes that are hashed with MD5 to obtain a cryptographic key.</p>
<figure>
    <img src="https://pt-corp.storage.yandexcloud.net/upload/corporate/ww-en/images/esc/article_316844/image18.png" alt="Handling of PL shellcode in the loader body (AES-128)"/>
    <figcaption>Figure 18. Handling of PL shellcode in the loader body (AES-128)</figcaption>
</figure>
<p class="weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_secondary-plus__47VrA">Older loader versions use Cryptography API: Next Generation (BCrypt* functions) in an equivalent way. They use AES-128 in CFB mode as the encryption algorithm.</p>
<p class="weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_secondary-plus__47VrA">The loaders that rely on external files have a similar code structure and one of two encryption types: ChaCha20 or AES-128-CBC. The file should contain PL shellcode of the same format as in the self-contained loader. The name depends on the specific sample and is encrypted with the algorithm used in it. It can contain a full file path (although we did not detect any such samples) or a relative path.</p>
<figure>
    <img src="https://pt-corp.storage.yandexcloud.net/upload/corporate/ww-en/images/esc/article_316844/image19.png" alt="Building the file name with PL shellcode"/>
    <figcaption>Figure 19. Building the file name with PL shellcode</figcaption>
</figure>
<p class="weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_secondary-plus__47VrA">Among all the loaders, we encountered three different shellcode payloads:</p>
<ul class="listStyles_list__w6p69 listStyles_ul__CFFG0 weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_secondary-plus__47VrA margin_xs__lb5TS">
<li>Crosswalk</li>
<li>Metasploit stager</li>
<li>Cobalt Strike Beacon</li>
</ul>
<h3 id="id2-3" class="break-words size_s__3_Wdt color_primary__z_39p margin_none__1sjaB">2.3 Attack examples</h3>
<h4 id="id2-3-1" class="break-words size_xs__89FKN color_primary__z_39p margin_none__1sjaB">2.3.1 An encrypted resume</h4>
<p class="weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_secondary-plus__47VrA">This malicious file is a RAR archive, electronic_resume.pdf.rar (025e053e329f7e5e930cc5aa8492a76e6bc61d5769aa614ec66088943bf77596), with two files:</p>
<figure>
    <img src="https://pt-corp.storage.yandexcloud.net/upload/corporate/ww-en/images/esc/article_316844/image20.png" alt="Contents of electronic_resume.pdf.rar"/>
    <figcaption>Figure 20. Contents of electronic_resume.pdf.rar</figcaption>
</figure>
<p class="weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_secondary-plus__47VrA">The first file might look like bait, but trying to open it in a PDF viewer gives an error, since it is practically a copy of the latter.</p>
<p class="weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_secondary-plus__47VrA">The file <em>Электронный читатель резюме.exe</em> (&quot;Electronic reader resume.exe&quot;) is an executable self-contained loader for PL shellcode. It contains Cobalt Strike Beacon as the payload.</p>
<figure>
    <img src="https://pt-corp.storage.yandexcloud.net/upload/corporate/ww-en/images/esc/article_316844/image21.png" alt="Configuration of Cobalt Strike Beacon"/>
    <figcaption>Figure 21. Configuration of Cobalt Strike Beacon</figcaption>
</figure>
<p class="weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_secondary-plus__47VrA">The archive was distributed on approximately June 1, 2020, from the IP address 66.42.48[.]186 and was available at hxxp://66.42.48[.]186:65500/electronic_resume.pdf.rar. The same IP address was used as C2 server.</p>
<p class="weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_secondary-plus__47VrA">The modification time of the archive files, as well as the date on which the archive was found the server, point to the attack being active in late May or early June. The Russian filenames suggest that the targets were Russian-speaking users.</p>
<h4 id="id2-3-2" class="break-words size_xs__89FKN color_primary__z_39p margin_none__1sjaB">2.3.2 I can&#x27;t breathe</h4>
<p class="weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_secondary-plus__47VrA">The attack is practically identical to the previous one: malware is distributed in a RAR archive video.rar (fc5c9c93781fbbac25d185ec8f920170503ec1eddfc623d2285a05d05d5552dc) and consists of two .exe files. The archive is available on June 1 on the same server at the address hxxp://66.42.48[.]186:65500/video.rar.</p>
<figure>
    <img src="https://pt-corp.storage.yandexcloud.net/upload/corporate/ww-en/images/esc/article_316844/image22.png" alt="Contents of video.rar"/>
    <figcaption>Figure 22. Contents of video.rar</figcaption>
</figure>
<p class="weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_secondary-plus__47VrA">The executable files are self-contained loaders of Cobalt Strike Beacon PL shellcode with a similar configuration and the same C2 server.</p>
<p class="weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_secondary-plus__47VrA">The bait is notable for the topic: the hackers were attempting to exploit U.S. protests related to the death of George Floyd. The main bait was a video with the name &quot;I can&#x27;t breathe-America&#x27;s Black Death protests that the riots continue to escalate and ignite America!.mp4&quot; involving reporting on protests in late May, 2020. Judging by the logo, the source of the video was Australian portal XKb, which releases news materials in Chinese.</p>
<figure>
    <img src="https://pt-corp.storage.yandexcloud.net/upload/corporate/ww-en/images/esc/article_316844/image23.png" alt="Still frame from the bait video"/>
    <figcaption>Figure 23. Still frame from the bait video</figcaption>
</figure>
<h4 id="id2-3-3" class="break-words size_xs__89FKN color_primary__z_39p margin_none__1sjaB">2.3.3 Chat transcript</h4>
<p class="weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_secondary-plus__47VrA">The archive <em>запись чата.7z</em> (&quot;chat transcript.7z&quot;) (e0b675302efc8c94e94b400a67bc627889bfdebb4f4dffdd68fdbc61d4cd03ae) contains three identical executable files with names resembling &quot;запись чата-1.png____________________________________.exe&quot; (&quot;chat transcript-1.png____________________________________.exe&quot;) in attacks again targeting Russian-speaking users.</p>
<figure>
    <img src="https://pt-corp.storage.yandexcloud.net/upload/corporate/ww-en/images/esc/article_316844/image24.png" alt="Contents of the archive, the name of which promises a &#x27;chat transcript&#x27;"/>
    <figcaption>Figure 24. Contents of the archive, the name of which promises a &quot;chat transcript&quot;</figcaption>
</figure>
<p class="weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_secondary-plus__47VrA">The malicious files are self-contained PL shellcode loaders, but the payload here is Crosswalk version 2.0.</p>
<p class="weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_secondary-plus__47VrA">Its configuration implies three ways to connect to the C2 server at 149.28.23[.]32:</p>
<ul class="listStyles_list__w6p69 listStyles_ul__CFFG0 weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_secondary-plus__47VrA margin_xs__lb5TS">
<li><p class="weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_secondary-plus__47VrA">Transport protocol 3, port 8443</p></li>
<li><p class="weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_secondary-plus__47VrA">Transport protocol 2, port 80</p></li>
<li><p class="weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_secondary-plus__47VrA">Transport protocol 1, port 8080</p></li>
</ul>
<figure>
    <img src="https://pt-corp.storage.yandexcloud.net/upload/corporate/ww-en/images/esc/article_316844/image25.png" alt="Fragment of the Crosswalk configuration"/>
    <figcaption>Figure 25. Fragment of the Crosswalk configuration</figcaption>
</figure>
<h2 id="id3" class="break-words size_m__P_9U_ color_primary__z_39p margin_none__1sjaB">3. Attacks on Russian game developers</h2>
<p class="weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_secondary-plus__47VrA">The Winnti group first became famous for its attacks on computer game developers. Such attacks continue today, and Russian companies are also among their targets.</p>
<h3 id="id3-1" class="break-words size_s__3_Wdt color_primary__z_39p margin_none__1sjaB">3.1 Unity3D Game Developer from St. Petersburg</h3>
<p class="weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_secondary-plus__47VrA">The attack is based on the archive Resume.rar (4d3ad3ff281a144d9a0a8ae5680f13e201ce1a6ba70e53a74510f0e41ae6a9e6), which contains just one file: CV.chm.</p>
<p class="weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_secondary-plus__47VrA">Running the file without security updates installed causes two windows to appear simultaneously: CHM help in HTML Help and a PDF document. They contain the same information: a curriculum vitae for the position of game developer or database manager at a St. Petersburg company.</p>
<p class="weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_secondary-plus__47VrA">The CV contains plausible contact information, with a St. Petersburg address, email address ending with &quot;@yandex.ru&quot;, and phone number starting with &quot;+7&quot; (Russia&#x27;s country code). The only obviously fake aspect is the phone number: 123-45-67.</p>
<figure>
    <img src="https://pt-corp.storage.yandexcloud.net/upload/corporate/ww-en/images/esc/article_316844/image26.png" alt="Result of opening the CHM file"/>
    <figcaption>Figure 26. Result of opening the CHM file</figcaption>
</figure>
<p class="weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_secondary-plus__47VrA">The PDF file opens due to the script pass.js, which is contained in the CHM file and referenced in the code of the HTML page.</p>
<figure>
    <img src="https://pt-corp.storage.yandexcloud.net/upload/corporate/ww-en/images/esc/article_316844/image27.png" alt="Reference to pass.js in HTML code"/>
    <figcaption>Figure 27. Reference to pass.js in HTML code</figcaption>
</figure>
<p class="weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_secondary-plus__47VrA">The script uses <a target="_blank" rel="nofollow noreferrer" href="https://twitter.com/ithurricanept/status/534993743196090368" class="linkStyles_links-gray__auxKT weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_links-gray__IEMoh linkStyles_underlined__4E3jf" title="https://twitter.com/ithurricanept/status/534993743196090368">a technique</a> for running an arbitrary command in a CHM file via an ActiveX object. This unpacks an HTML help file to the folder C:\Users\Public for launching the next stage of the infection: the file resume.exe, which is also embedded inside the CHM file.</p>
<figure>
    <img src="https://pt-corp.storage.yandexcloud.net/upload/corporate/ww-en/images/esc/article_316844/image28.png" alt="Deobfuscated script pass.js"/>
    <figcaption>Figure 28. Deobfuscated script pass.js</figcaption>
</figure>
<p class="weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_secondary-plus__47VrA">resume.exe is an advanced shellcode injector of which we had encountered only one sample as of the writing of this article. Before it gets down to business, this malware, like many other samples we have seen from Winnti, checks the current year. Current processes are checked and the malware will not run if any of the following are active: ollydbg.exe|ProcessHacker.exe|Fiddler.exe|windbg.exe|tcpview.exe|idaq.exe|idaq64.exe|tcpdump.exe|Wireshark.exe.</p>
<p class="weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_secondary-plus__47VrA">On first launch, shellcode will be taken from MyResume.pdf; on subsequent launches, winness.config is the shellcode source.</p>
<figure>
    <img src="https://pt-corp.storage.yandexcloud.net/upload/corporate/ww-en/images/esc/article_316844/image29.png" alt="Main function in resume.exe"/>
    <figcaption>Figure 29. Main function in resume.exe</figcaption>
</figure>
<p class="weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_secondary-plus__47VrA">MyResume.pdf is unpacked from the CHM file. Data read by resume.exe has been added to the end of the PDF file. If the user opens it directly, a message warns that the document is password-protected.</p>
<figure>
    <img src="https://pt-corp.storage.yandexcloud.net/upload/corporate/ww-en/images/esc/article_316844/image30.png" alt="MyResume.pdf, as viewed in Adobe Acrobat Reader"/>
    <figcaption>Figure 30. MyResume.pdf, as viewed in Adobe Acrobat Reader</figcaption>
</figure>
<p class="weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_secondary-plus__47VrA">Compared to the PL shellcode, the data structure is more complex and contains the following:</p>
<ul class="listStyles_list__w6p69 listStyles_ul__CFFG0 weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_secondary-plus__47VrA margin_xs__lb5TS">
<li>ROR-13 hash of data starting from byte 0x24 (0x20, 4 bytes)</li>
<li>Nonce for algorithm ChaCha20 (0x24, 12 bytes)</li>
<li>ChaCha20-encrypted text (0x30):
<ul class="listStyles_list__w6p69 listStyles_ul__CFFG0 weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_secondary-plus__47VrA margin_xs__lb5TS">
<li>Name of PDF file (+0x0)</li>
<li>Size of PDF file (+0x20)</li>
<li>Size of auxiliary shellcode (+0x24)</li>
<li>Size of main shellcode (+0x28)</li>
<li>Constant 0xE839E900 (+0x2C)</li>
<li>PDF file</li>
<li>Auxiliary shellcode</li>
<li>Main shellcode</li>
</ul></li>
</ul>
<p class="weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_secondary-plus__47VrA">On first launch of resume.exe, the encrypted portion of the data is decrypted (the key is hard-coded in the executable) and three sections are extracted (PDF, auxiliary shellcode, and main shellcode). The PDF file is saved with a name resembling _797918755_true.pdf in a temporary folder. It then opens for the user (the second window in the screenshot on Figure 26, next to HTML Help).</p>
<figure>
    <img src="https://pt-corp.storage.yandexcloud.net/upload/corporate/ww-en/images/esc/article_316844/image31.png" alt="resume.exe: actions on first launch"/>
    <figcaption>Figure 31. resume.exe: actions on first launch</figcaption>
</figure>
<p class="weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_secondary-plus__47VrA">The payload runs in a new process %windir%\System32\spoolsv.exe, into which the main shellcode is injected: Cobalt Strike Beacon with C2 address 149.28.84[.]98.</p>
<p class="weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_secondary-plus__47VrA">Injection occurs by creating a section via a ZwCreateSection call, getting access to it from the parent and child processes via ZwMapViewOfSection calls, copying shellcode to the section, and placing a jump to the shellcode at the entry point for spoolsv.exe.</p>
<p class="weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_secondary-plus__47VrA">For persistence, resume.exe (under the name winness.exe) is copied to the folder %appdata%\Microsoft\AddIns\ and the main shellcode is re-encrypted and saved in the same location, with the name winness.config. To ensure autostart, auxiliary shellcode writes the file svchost.bat, which transfers control to winness.exe, to the startup folder. For avoiding detection at this stage, the auxiliary shellcode is injected in a similar way into spoolsv.exe, independently loads the necessary functions, and writes to file in a separate thread.</p>
<p class="weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_secondary-plus__47VrA">When winness.exe runs after a restart, the main shellcode is decrypted from winness.config and injected into spoolsv.exe in exactly the same way.</p>
<h3 id="id3-2" class="break-words size_s__3_Wdt color_primary__z_39p margin_none__1sjaB">3.2 HFS with a surprise</h3>
<figure>
    <img src="https://pt-corp.storage.yandexcloud.net/upload/corporate/ww-en/images/esc/article_316844/image32.png" alt="HFS server on Winnti infrastructure"/>
    <figcaption>Figure 32. HFS server on Winnti infrastructure</figcaption>
</figure>
<p class="weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_secondary-plus__47VrA">On June 23, 2020, while investigating Winnti network infrastructure, we detected an active <a target="_blank" rel="nofollow noreferrer" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/HTTP_File_Server" class="linkStyles_links-gray__auxKT weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_links-gray__IEMoh linkStyles_underlined__4E3jf" title="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/HTTP_File_Server">HttpFileServer</a> on one of the active C2 servers. Four images were there for all to see: an email icon, screenshot from a game with Russian text, screenshot of the site of a game development company, and a screenshot of information about vulnerability <a target="_blank" rel="nofollow noreferrer" href="https://portal.msrc.microsoft.com/en-us/security-guidance/advisory/CVE-2020-0796" class="linkStyles_links-gray__auxKT weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_links-gray__IEMoh linkStyles_underlined__4E3jf" title="https://portal.msrc.microsoft.com/en-us/security-guidance/advisory/CVE-2020-0796">CVE-2020-0796</a> from the Microsoft website.</p>
<figure>
    <img src="https://pt-corp.storage.yandexcloud.net/upload/corporate/ww-en/images/esc/article_316844/image33.png" alt="13524222881554126454-128.png"/>
    <figcaption>Figure 33. 13524222881554126454-128.png</figcaption>
</figure>
<figure>
    <img src="https://pt-corp.storage.yandexcloud.net/upload/corporate/ww-en/images/esc/article_316844/image34.png" alt="EaVpPBNXgAE8s3r.jpg"/>
    <figcaption>Figure 34. EaVpPBNXgAE8s3r.jpg</figcaption>
</figure>
<figure>
    <img src="https://pt-corp.storage.yandexcloud.net/upload/corporate/ww-en/images/esc/article_316844/image35.png" alt="website_battlestategames.png"/>
    <figcaption>Figure 35. website_battlestategames.png</figcaption>
</figure>
<figure>
    <img src="https://pt-corp.storage.yandexcloud.net/upload/corporate/ww-en/images/esc/article_316844/image36.png" alt="windows_update.png"/>
    <figcaption>Figure 36. windows_update.png</figcaption>
</figure>
<p class="weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_secondary-plus__47VrA">The screenshots related to Battlestate Games, the St. Petersburg-based developer of <em>Escape from Tarkov.</em></p>
<p class="weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_secondary-plus__47VrA">Almost two months later, on August 20, 2020, the file CV.pdf____________________________________________________________.exe (e886caba3fea000a7de8948c4de0f9b5857f0baef6cf905a2c53641dbbc0277c) was uploaded to VirusTotal. This file is a self-contained loader for Cobalt Strike Beacon PL shellcode.</p>
<p class="weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_secondary-plus__47VrA">Its C2 server is interesting: update.facebookdocs[.]com.</p>
<p class="weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_secondary-plus__47VrA">We discovered that the main domain facebookdocs[.]com hosted a copy of the official site of Battlestate Games: www.battlestategames.com. Via an associated C2 IP address (108.61.214[.]194), we found an equivalent page on the phishing domain www.battllestategames[.]com (note the double &quot;l&quot;).</p>
<figure>
    <img src="https://pt-corp.storage.yandexcloud.net/upload/corporate/ww-en/images/esc/article_316844/image37.png" alt="Copy of the official Battlestate Games site"/>
    <figcaption>Figure 37. Copy of the official Battlestate Games site</figcaption>
</figure>
<p class="weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_secondary-plus__47VrA">When used as C2 servers, such domains give attackers the ability to mask malicious traffic as legitimate activity within the company.</p>
<p class="weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_secondary-plus__47VrA">The combination of these two finds makes us think that we detected traces of preparation for, and subsequent successful implementation of, an attack on Battlestate Games.</p>
<p class="weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_secondary-plus__47VrA">Moreover, the match between the job listing for Unity3D developer (as seen in the screenshot from the official site) and contents of the curriculum vitae in the file CV.chm (as described in the previous section), considering how closely they matched in time as well as the company and &quot;applicant&quot; both being located in St. Petersburg, suggests a connection between these attacks. Most likely, the CHM file attack was used at the beginning stage of the breach, although we do not have solid confirmation for this.</p>
<p class="weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_secondary-plus__47VrA">Use of typosquatting domains for C2 servers is typical of Winnti and has been described in a <a target="_blank" rel="nofollow noreferrer" href="https://media.kasperskycontenthub.com/wp-content/uploads/sites/43/2018/03/20134508/winnti-more-than-just-a-game-130410.pdf" class="linkStyles_links-gray__auxKT weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_links-gray__IEMoh linkStyles_underlined__4E3jf" title="https://media.kasperskycontenthub.com/wp-content/uploads/sites/43/2018/03/20134508/winnti-more-than-just-a-game-130410.pdf">Kaspersky report</a>.</p>
<p class="weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_secondary-plus__47VrA">Battlestate Games received all of the information uncovered by our investigation into the suspected attack.</p>
<h2 id="id4" class="break-words size_m__P_9U_ color_primary__z_39p margin_none__1sjaB">4. A purloined certificate</h2>
<p class="weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_secondary-plus__47VrA">Another favorite Winnti technique is theft of certificates for code signing. Compromised certificates are used to sign malicious files intended for future attacks.</p>
<p class="weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_secondary-plus__47VrA">We found one such certificate belonging to Taiwanese company Zealot Digital:</p>
<div class="bg-skin-content-general-reversed laptop:rounded-xl rounded-lg py-8 pl-5 pr-8 text-[#ABB2BF] HtmlBlock_code__Ng4fC"><div class="pr-15 relative flex gap-5"><pre class="w-full whitespace-pre-wrap break-words"><div class="code">Name:           ZEALOT DIGITAL INTERNATIONAL CORPORATION
Issuer:         GlobalSign CodeSigning CA - SHA256 - G2
Valid From:     07:43 AM 08/20/2015
Valid To:       07:43 AM 09/19/2016
Valid Usage:    Code Signing
Algorithm:      sha256RSA
Thumbprint:     91e256ac753efe79927db468a5fa60cb8a835ba5
Serial Number:  112195a147c06211d2c4b82b627e3d07bf09</div></pre><div class="absolute bottom-0 right-0" data-theme-direction="reversed"><button type="button" class="IconButton_icon__uMEG7 IconButton_sm__bK6zi IconButton_outlined__kMeaW"><div class="Icon_icon__IyoOP Icon_sm__5FkXp"><svg role="img" focusable="false" aria-hidden="true" viewBox="0 0 20 20" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" fill="none"><path stroke="currentColor" d="M6 3a2 2 0 0 1 2-2h8a2 2 0 0 1 2 2v10a2 2 0 0 1-2 2H8a2 2 0 0 1-2-2V3Z"></path><path stroke="currentColor" d="M14 17a2 2 0 0 1-2 2H4a2 2 0 0 1-2-2V7a2 2 0 0 1 2-2"></path></svg></div></button></div></div></div>
<p class="weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_secondary-plus__47VrA">The files signed with it were predominantly used in attacks on organizations in Hong Kong. They include Crosswalk and Metasploit injectors, the juicy-potato utility, and samples of FunnySwitch and ShadowPad.</p>
<h2 id="id5" class="break-words size_m__P_9U_ color_primary__z_39p margin_none__1sjaB">5. FunnySwitch</h2>
<p class="weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_secondary-plus__47VrA">Among the files signed with the Zealot Digital certificate, we discovered two samples of malware containing a previously unknown backdoor. We have called it FunnySwitch, based on the name of the library and one of the key classes. The backdoor is written in .NET and can send system information as well as run arbitrary JScript code, with support for six different connection types, including the ability to accept incoming connections. One of its distinguishing features is the ability to act as message relay between different copies of the backdoor and a C2 server.</p>
<h3 id="id5-1" class="break-words size_s__3_Wdt color_primary__z_39p margin_none__1sjaB">5.1 Unpacking</h3>
<p class="weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_secondary-plus__47VrA">The attack in question starts with the SFX archive x32.exe (2063fae36db936de23eb728bcf3f8a5572f83645786c2a0a5529c71d8447a9af).</p>
<figure>
    <img src="https://pt-corp.storage.yandexcloud.net/upload/corporate/ww-en/images/esc/article_316844/image38.png" alt="Contents of the archive x32.exe"/>
    <figcaption>Figure 38. Contents of the archive x32.exe</figcaption>
</figure>
<p class="weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_secondary-plus__47VrA">The archive unpacks three files (1.vbs, n3.exe, and p3.exe) into the folder c:\programdata, after which the extracted VBS script runs both executables.</p>
<p class="weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_secondary-plus__47VrA">The files n3.exe and p3.exe are identical and inject shellcode into the process explorer.exe. The only difference between them is the final bytes of the shellcode they inject, which contain the XML configuration. In one case, the proxy server 168.106.1[.]1 is specified there in addition:</p>
<div class="bg-skin-content-general-reversed laptop:rounded-xl rounded-lg py-8 pl-5 pr-8 text-[#ABB2BF] HtmlBlock_code__Ng4fC"><div class="pr-15 relative flex gap-5"><pre class="w-full whitespace-pre-wrap break-words"><div class="code">&lt;?xml version=&quot;1.0&quot; encoding=&quot;utf-8&quot;?&gt;
&lt;Config Group=&quot;aa&quot; Password=&quot;test&quot; StartTime=&quot;0&quot; EndTime=&quot;24&quot; WeekDays=&quot;0,1,2,3,4,5,6&quot;&gt;
    &lt;HttpConnector url=&quot;http://db311secsd.kasprsky[.]info/config/&quot; proxy=&quot;http://168.106.1[.]1/&quot; interval=&quot;30-60&quot;/&gt;
&lt;/Config&gt;
&lt;?xml version=&quot;1.0&quot; encoding=&quot;utf-8&quot;?&gt;
&lt;Config Group=&quot;aa&quot; Password=&quot;test&quot; StartTime=&quot;0&quot; EndTime=&quot;24&quot; WeekDays=&quot;0,1,2,3,4,5,6&quot;&gt;
    &lt;HttpConnector url=&quot;http://db311secsd.kasprsky[.]info/config/&quot; interval=&quot;30-60&quot;/&gt;
&lt;/Config&gt;</div></pre><div class="absolute bottom-0 right-0" data-theme-direction="reversed"><button type="button" class="IconButton_icon__uMEG7 IconButton_sm__bK6zi IconButton_outlined__kMeaW"><div class="Icon_icon__IyoOP Icon_sm__5FkXp"><svg role="img" focusable="false" aria-hidden="true" viewBox="0 0 20 20" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" fill="none"><path stroke="currentColor" d="M6 3a2 2 0 0 1 2-2h8a2 2 0 0 1 2 2v10a2 2 0 0 1-2 2H8a2 2 0 0 1-2-2V3Z"></path><path stroke="currentColor" d="M14 17a2 2 0 0 1-2 2H4a2 2 0 0 1-2-2V7a2 2 0 0 1 2-2"></path></svg></div></button></div></div></div>
<p class="weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_secondary-plus__47VrA">A subdomain of kasprsky[.]info, db311secsd.kasprsky[.]info, is the C2 domain. Interestingly, several of its other subdomains <a target="_blank" rel="nofollow noreferrer" href="https://www.alaha.org/wp-content/uploads/2020/05/Cyber-Alert.pdf" class="linkStyles_links-gray__auxKT weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_links-gray__IEMoh linkStyles_underlined__4E3jf" title="https://www.alaha.org/wp-content/uploads/2020/05/Cyber-Alert.pdf">are mentioned in an FBI report</a>. It dates to May 21, 2020, and warns of attacks on organizations linked to COVID-19 research.</p>
<p class="weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_secondary-plus__47VrA">The job of the shellcode is to launch and execute a method from the .NET assembly located immediately after its code. To do so, it gets a reference to the ICorRuntimeHost interface, which it uses to run <a target="_blank" rel="nofollow noreferrer" href="https://docs.microsoft.com/en-us/dotnet/standard/clr" class="linkStyles_links-gray__auxKT weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_links-gray__IEMoh linkStyles_underlined__4E3jf" title="https://docs.microsoft.com/en-us/dotnet/standard/clr">CLR</a> and create an <a target="_blank" rel="nofollow noreferrer" href="https://docs.microsoft.com/en-us/dotnet/api/system.appdomain" class="linkStyles_links-gray__auxKT weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_links-gray__IEMoh linkStyles_underlined__4E3jf" title="https://docs.microsoft.com/en-us/dotnet/api/system.appdomain">AppDomain</a> object. The contents of the assembly are loaded into the newly created domain. Reflection is used to run the static method Funny.Core.Run(xml_config), to which the XML configuration is passed.</p>
<figure>
    <img src="https://pt-corp.storage.yandexcloud.net/upload/corporate/ww-en/images/esc/article_316844/image39.png" alt="Calling a method from the .NET assembly"/>
    <figcaption>Figure 39. Calling a method from the .NET assembly</figcaption>
</figure>
<p class="weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_secondary-plus__47VrA">The assembly is the library Funny.dll with obfuscation by ConfuserEx.</p>
<h3 id="id5-2" class="break-words size_s__3_Wdt color_primary__z_39p margin_none__1sjaB">5.2 Funny.dll</h3>
<p class="weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_secondary-plus__47VrA">The backdoor starts by parsing the configuration. Its root element may contain the following fields:</p>
<ul class="listStyles_list__w6p69 listStyles_ul__CFFG0 weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_secondary-plus__47VrA margin_xs__lb5TS">
<li>Debug is the flag for enabling debug logging</li>
<li>Group is an arbitrary string sent together with system information.</li>
<li>Password is the key used to encrypt messages.</li>
<li>ID identifies the relay (if not present in the configuration, the GUID is used instead).</li>
<li>StartTime, EndTime, and WeekDays restrict the times and days when the backdoor may function</li>
</ul>
<p class="weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_secondary-plus__47VrA">The <code>&lt;Config&gt;</code> element may contain an arbitrary number of elements describing various types of connectors:</p>
<ul class="listStyles_list__w6p69 listStyles_ul__CFFG0 weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_secondary-plus__47VrA margin_xs__lb5TS">
<li><p class="weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_secondary-plus__47VrA">TcpConnector and TcpBindConnector are classes responsible for connecting over TCP as client and server.</p>
	<p class="weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_secondary-plus__47VrA">They have two parameters in common: <code>address</code> and <code>port</code> (by default, 38001). TcpConnector also has the <code>parameter</code> interval, which indicates how long to wait before trying to reconnect.</p></li>
<li><p class="weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_secondary-plus__47VrA">HttpConnector and HttpBindConnector are HTTP client with support for proxy and HTTP server.</p>
	<p class="weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_secondary-plus__47VrA">Supported client parameters: <code>url</code> – address to connect to, <code>interval</code> – same as at TcpConnector, <code>proxy</code> and <code>cred</code> – proxy server address and credentials. Server parameters: <code>url</code> – list of prefixes on which it will run and <code>timeout</code> – client timeout.</p>
<p class="weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_secondary-plus__47VrA">The standard classes HttpWebRequest and HttpListener from .NET Framework are used for client and server implementations. Both HTTP and HTTPS are supported: if no SSL certificate is configured for the port on which the server is running, it will be launched with CN = <code>Environment.MachineName + &quot;.local.domain&quot;</code>. The client, in turn, ignores certificate validation.</p>
</li>
<li><p class="weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_secondary-plus__47VrA">RPCConnector and RPCBindConnector are classes that allow setting up a connection via a Named Pipe. They take a single parameter, <code>name</code>, which is the name of the connection.</p></li>
</ul>
<p class="weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_secondary-plus__47VrA">TcpBindConnector and HttpBindConnector support simultaneous connections for multiple clients.</p>
<p class="weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_secondary-plus__47VrA">For the network connectors to work, the backdoor adds an allow rule to Windows Firewall with the name &quot;Core Networking ― IPv4&quot; for its executable module.</p>
<figure>
    <img src="https://pt-corp.storage.yandexcloud.net/upload/corporate/ww-en/images/esc/article_316844/image40.png" alt="Code for adding Windows Firewall rules"/>
    <figcaption>Figure 40. Code for adding Windows Firewall rules</figcaption>
</figure>
<p class="weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_secondary-plus__47VrA">Just like with Crosswalk, there are multiple levels of the protocol: in this case, transport, network, and application.</p>
<h4 id="id5-2-1" class="break-words size_xs__89FKN color_primary__z_39p margin_none__1sjaB">5.2.1 Transport protocols</h4>
<ol class="listStyles_list__w6p69 listStyles_ol__ZnTfe weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_secondary-plus__47VrA margin_xs__lb5TS">
<li>TCP
	<p class="weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_secondary-plus__47VrA">TCP supports three types of messages: PingMessage (0x1), PongMessage (0x2), and DataMessage (0x3). The first two monitor the connection and are relevant only at the TcpConnector/TcpBindConnector level. DataMessage contains network-level data.</p>
<p class="weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_secondary-plus__47VrA">Messages consist of a signature (4 bytes), encrypted header (16 bytes), and optional data.</p>
<p class="weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_secondary-plus__47VrA">The signature is three random bytes followed by their sum with modulo 256. Incoming messages with an invalid signature are discarded.</p>
<p class="weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_secondary-plus__47VrA">The header contains the data size (4 bytes) and byte indicating the message type (0x1, 0x2, or 0x3).</p>
<p class="weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_secondary-plus__47VrA">It is encrypted with AES-256-CBC; the key and IV are taken from the MD5 of the key string. The backdoor uses this encryption method in other cases as well, which is why we refer to it as &quot;standard&quot; in the text that follows. The key string in this case is &quot;tcp_encrypted&quot;.</p>
<figure>
    <img src="https://pt-corp.storage.yandexcloud.net/upload/corporate/ww-en/images/esc/article_316844/image41.png" alt="Standard encryption in FunnySwitch"/>
    <figcaption>Figure 41. Standard encryption in FunnySwitch</figcaption>
</figure>
</li>
<li>HTTP with long polling
	<p class="weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_secondary-plus__47VrA">There are three types of requests: GET &quot;connect&quot;, GET &quot;pull&quot;, and POST &quot;push&quot;. To start transferring data, the client must connect by sending a GET request to a URL from the configuration and provide a special cookie value.</p>
<p class="weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_secondary-plus__47VrA">The cookie name is eight random characters. The value is an encrypted Base64 string containing the session GUID and operation name (&quot;connect&quot;). The string is encrypted in the standard way with the key &quot;http&quot;.</p>
<p class="weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_secondary-plus__47VrA">The client then constantly sends GET requests with pull operations. In response, the server returns the relevant array of messages for the client or, if no new messages have arrived in the last 10 seconds, an empty response. Client–server messages are periodically sent as an array as well, for which a POST request with push operation is used.</p>
<figure>
    <img src="https://pt-corp.storage.yandexcloud.net/upload/corporate/ww-en/images/esc/article_316844/image42.png" alt="FunnySwitch connect and pull requests"/>
    <figcaption>Figure 42. FunnySwitch connect and pull requests</figcaption>
</figure>
<p class="weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_secondary-plus__47VrA">The special class MsgPack class, which implements a custom serialization protocol, unpacks the array and other primitive types.</p>
</li>
<li>RPC (Pipe)
	<p class="weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_secondary-plus__47VrA">Similar to TCP, except for the absence of connection monitoring.</p>
</li>
</ol>
<h4 id="id5-2-2" class="break-words size_xs__89FKN color_primary__z_39p margin_none__1sjaB">5.2.2 Network-level protocol</h4>
<figure>
    <img src="https://pt-corp.storage.yandexcloud.net/upload/corporate/ww-en/images/esc/article_316844/image43.png" alt="Function for processing incoming network-level communications"/>
    <figcaption>Figure 43. Function for processing incoming network-level communications</figcaption>
</figure>
<p class="weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_secondary-plus__47VrA">All messages at this level are encrypted in the backdoor&#x27;s standard way, with the key string &quot;commonkey&quot;.</p>
<p class="weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_secondary-plus__47VrA">Messages are an array of three or four elements:</p>
<ul class="listStyles_list__w6p69 listStyles_ul__CFFG0 weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_secondary-plus__47VrA margin_xs__lb5TS">
<li>Message type (&quot;hello_request&quot;, &quot;hello_response&quot;, &quot;message&quot;, &quot;error&quot;)</li>
<li>Source serialized array</li>
<li>Destination serialized array</li>
<li>Payload (application-level data)</li>
</ul>
<p class="weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_secondary-plus__47VrA">The <em>MsgPack</em> class is also used for serialization. The Source and Destination arrays contain the IDs of the relays through which the message has already passed and the IDs of the routers through it should be delivered to the recipient.</p>
<p class="weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_secondary-plus__47VrA">The bodies of hello_request and hello_response messages contain information about the sender&#x27;s system. When one of these messages is received, the relay saves data about the sender ID, used connector instance and system data. These message types are used to establish a direct connection between relays.</p>
<p class="weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_secondary-plus__47VrA">Messages of the &quot;message&quot; type (ones that are not hello_request, hello_response, or error) can be passed via several relays. If its Destination field contains only the ID of the current instance, it will be handled locally; if not, it will be sent to the next relay in the list. For connecting to the next instance, it uses the connector that was saved when exchanging hello_request and hello_response messages.</p>
<p class="weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_secondary-plus__47VrA">The backdoor collects the following system information:</p>
<ul class="listStyles_list__w6p69 listStyles_ul__CFFG0 weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_secondary-plus__47VrA margin_xs__lb5TS">
<li>Values of the registry keys ProductName and CSDVersion from HKLM\SOFTWARE\Microsoft\Windows NT\CurrentVersion</li>
<li>Whether the OS is 32-bit or 64-bit</li>
<li>List of IP addresses</li>
<li>Computer name</li>
<li>Username and workgroup</li>
<li>Name of running module</li>
<li>PID</li>
<li>MAC addresses of network adapters</li>
<li>Value of the Group attribute in the XML configuration</li>
</ul>
<h4 id="id5-2-3" class="break-words size_xs__89FKN color_primary__z_39p margin_none__1sjaB">5.2.3 Application-level protocol</h4>
<p class="weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_secondary-plus__47VrA">At the application level, data is encrypted in the standard way using the value of the Password attribute from the configuration. If no such value exists, the key string is &quot;test&quot;. Data is compressed with GZip prior to encryption.</p>
<p class="weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_secondary-plus__47VrA">After decryption and decompression, the payload is an array (packed <em>MsgPack</em>) consisting of one or two elements: a string with the name of a command and optional array of bytes (data for the command). These elements, in turn, contain another serialized array, which contains a message string ID (which will be used to send the result of the command) plus the data for the command.</p>
<h4 id="id5-2-4" class="break-words size_xs__89FKN color_primary__z_39p margin_none__1sjaB">5.2.4 Supported commands</h4>
<div class="table-container">
    <table class="table-article table-article_striped">
<thead>
<tr>
<th>Command</th>
<th>Description</th>
</tr>
</thead>
<tbody>
<tr>
<td>invoke</td>
<td><p class="weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_secondary-plus__47VrA">Run JScript code and get the result. Implementation was separated out into a JSCore .NET assembly, which is dynamically loaded from a Base64 constant defined in the main assembly.</p>
<figure>
    <img src="https://pt-corp.storage.yandexcloud.net/upload/corporate/ww-en/images/esc/article_316844/image44.png" alt="Loading the Funny.Eval class from the JSCore assembly"/>
    <figcaption>Figure 44. Loading the Funny.Eval class from the JSCore assembly</figcaption>
</figure>
<p class="weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_secondary-plus__47VrA">Code execution is accomplished with classes from the Microsoft.JScript namespace.</p>
<figure>
    <img src="https://pt-corp.storage.yandexcloud.net/upload/corporate/ww-en/images/esc/article_316844/image45.png" alt="Code fragments from the Funny.Eval class"/>
    <figcaption>Figure 45. Code fragments from the Funny.Eval class</figcaption>
</figure></td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td>connect</td>
<td>Takes an XML string with connector configuration and creates the corresponding object.</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td>update</td>
<td>Packs a response containing the IDs of relays connected to the current copy, together with their system information.</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td>query</td>
<td>Collects the configuration of active connector instances other than the RPCConnector and RPCBindConnector classes.</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td>remove</td>
<td>Removes the specified connector.</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td>createStream</td>
<td>Creates a message queue with the indicated name. The queue connects with the sender of the createStream command.</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td>closeStream</td>
<td>Deletes the named message queue.</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td>sendStream</td>
<td>Adds a message (byte array) to the queue with the specified name.</td>
</tr>
</tbody>
</table>
</div>
<p class="weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_secondary-plus__47VrA">The result of execution of each command is returned to the sender via the invoke-response command.</p>
